ISAAC: 1. Vector Compression & Compilation
Test patterns (vectors) start as .fvec text files, which are compiled by a Rust host tool (fbc-vec) into a highly compressed .fbc binary format. The compression strategy achieves extremely high ratios (e.g., 710x for repetitive patterns) by replacing full vectors with compact opcodes:

VECTOR_ZERO (1 byte): All pins driven low.
VECTOR_ONES (1 byte): All pins driven high.
VECTOR_RUN (3 bytes): Repeat the previous vector 
N
 times.
VECTOR_SPARSE (2+N bytes): XOR mask for small toggle changes.
VECTOR_FULL (21 bytes): The complete raw 160-bit vector when there's no pattern.
2. Raw Ethernet Protocol
To maximize bandwidth and minimize latency, the system bypasses the standard TCP/IP stack entirely.

It implements a custom Raw Ethernet Protocol via the Zynq Gigabit Ethernet MAC (GEM).
Packets are wrapped directly in Ethernet frames using a custom EtherType of 0x88B5.
The host streams the .fbc payloads, and the firmware (firmware/src/net.rs) parses the frames directly using the source/destination MAC addresses.
3. Firmware Decompression
When the compressed .fbc payload is received over Ethernet, the firmware (FbcLoader and FbcDecompressor) steps in:

It iterates over the compressed opcodes and expands them into 32-byte FPGA bytecode instruction words.
Each 32-byte word contains the 64-bit opcode/flag data and a 128-bit payload containing the pin states. It is strictly padded to 32 bytes to ensure aligned bursts into the FPGA without splitting instructions.
4. Data Transfer (DMA to RTL)
To stream the decompressed bytecode to the FPGA RTL (fbc_decoder.v), the system uses AXI DMA (firmware/src/dma.rs):

Decompressed instructions are placed into On-Chip Memory (OCM) to guarantee cache coherency (defined in firmware/link.ld).
The driver sets up a Memory-Mapped to Stream (MM2S) AXI DMA transfer.
The DMA controller blasts the instructions into the FPGA over an AXI4-Stream interface.
Once the FPGA receives the stream, the RTL executes the vector patterns on the fast pins (e.g., 1-cycle latency GPIOs) using the configured vector clock frequency.
CC: Now I see the real architecture. Let me map it directly:

| FBC Pipeline | two3 Training Pipeline |
|---|---|
| `.fvec` text → `.fbc` compressed binary | Raw text → compressed training format |
| Opcodes: ZERO, ONES, RUN, SPARSE, FULL | Opcodes: REPEAT_TOKEN, COMMON_NGRAM, SKIP_EASY, RARE_SEQUENCE, RAW_BYTES |
| Raw Ethernet bypasses TCP/IP stack | Direct VRAM load bypasses CPU staging |
| Firmware decompresses → 32-byte FPGA words | GPU kernel decompresses → token sequences |
| AXI DMA streams to RTL, zero-copy | Device memory, zero-copy forward pass |
| 1-cycle latency execution | Single kernel launch per batch |

The key insight from FBC: **the compression isn't just storage — it's an execution format**. The opcodes tell the FPGA *what to do*, not just what data to hold. `VECTOR_RUN` doesn't store N copies of the vector, it stores "do this N times" as a 3-byte instruction.

For training data, the same principle: don't store "the the the" as 12 bytes. Store `COMMON_NGRAM(id=THE) REPEAT(3)`. The GPU decompressor kernel expands it inline during the forward pass. The compression ratio on Shakespeare would be massive — it's repetitive text with recurring patterns.

And the hard-example replay maps to the FBC retry pattern. When a vector fails on the DUT, the controller re-queues it. When a training sequence has high loss, it goes into the replay buffer in VRAM. The GPU processes replay sequences whenever the main data stream has a gap.

The 32-byte aligned instruction words are the detail that matters — the FPGA needs aligned bursts. The GPU needs coalesced memory access. Same constraint, same solution: pad your format to the hardware's natural width.

ISAAC: Isaac is asking a concrete engineering question. Where does the onetwo_parse encoding fit into the two3 training pipeline?

Let me think about this clearly.

Current two3 pipeline:

Input: raw byte (0-255)
Embedding: byte → learned 256×dim float vector
Ternary layers: QKV projections, MoE, etc.
Output: logits over 256 bytes
The onetwo_parse gives us a 4096-bit structural fingerprint for a WINDOW of bytes. The question is how this plugs into two3.

Options:

Replace the embedding layer: Instead of looking up byte index in a learned embedding table, run onetwo_parse on the context window up to that position and use the fingerprint as the input vector.
The challenge is that onetwo_parse operates on a window rather than individual bytes, and computing it at every position gets expensive. I could instead replace the entire input representation—but then the model would be predicting fingerprints rather than bytes, which changes the task fundamentally. Another approach is augmenting the byte embeddings by concatenating structural features from onetwo_parse, giving the model richer context. Or I could treat the fingerprint itself as the tokenizer output, where each training position receives the onetwo_parse fingerprint of the preceding window, replacing the embedding lookup entirely and having the model's first layer project from the fingerprint dimensionality.

But thinking back to what Isaac mentioned about the model speaking natively in the structural paradigm—the vocabulary should actually be these fingerprints themselves. The question is how this fits into the existing two3 training loop, since the IBC compiler and onetwo_parse already exist in their respective frameworks. The answer seems to be that the fingerprint replaces the embedding layer entirely: instead of looking up a learned embedding for each byte, the model receives the structural fingerprint directly and projects from there.

The practical implementation would be straightforward—pre-compute the fingerprints for every position in the training data, use them as the input vectors directly, and let the ternary layers handle the routing. This eliminates the embedding layer's parameters entirely, making the model more efficient while keeping the same routing behavior. state at position t

But there's a cost: onetwo_parse is expensive per position. For a 128-token sequence, you'd run onetwo_parse 128 times with growing windows. That's O(n²) in the sequence length.

Solution: pre-compute all fingerprints offline. Store them alongside the training data. During training, just load the pre-computed fingerprint instead of computing it.

That's exactly what the IBC compiler does — it pre-compiles the structural information. The .ibc file contains pre-computed pinout vectors for each token position.

So the flow is:

Phase 1 compiler (ibc_compile) runs on corpus → outputs .ibc with pinout table + compressed token sequence
two3 data loader reads .ibc → for each position, looks up the pinout vector from the table
The pinout vector enters the model instead of a byte embedding
The model's first layer should treat the four dimensions of the pinout vector separately rather than flattening them — each dimension gets its own projection pathway, then they combine. This lets the model learn which dimensions actually matter for language modeling, since we've already seen that X and Y carry more signal than Z and T.

Actually, the fingerprint itself is the embedding. There's no need for a separate embedding table at all — the structural fingerprint of the context is the representation. Pre-compute it, load it, done.

Done
Right. Concrete wiring.

Current two3 forward pass:

byte_in → embed[byte_in] → ternary layers → logits
           ↑ learned, 256 × dim
The embedding is where the model wastes capacity learning structure. Replace it:

context window → onetwo_parse → [X|Y|Z|T] → ternary layers → logits
                  ↑ free, 4096 bits            ↑ only learns routing
The IBC compiler you already built does the pre-computation. Run it once on the corpus. Every position in the training data gets its fingerprint stored in the .ibc file. During training, the data loader reads the pre-computed fingerprint instead of a byte index. No embed[byte_val * dim] lookup. The fingerprint IS the embedding.

And the dimensional observation we just proved — X and Y carry signal, Z and T are noise at this scale — that tells you how to wire the first projection layer. Not one flat 4096→dim projection. Four separate paths:

X (1024 bits) → Wx (ternary, small) → hx
Y (1024 bits) → Wy (ternary, small) → hy
Z (1024 bits) → Wz (ternary, small) → hz
T (1024 bits) → Wt (ternary, small) → ht
                                        ↓
                                   concat → ternary layers
Four ternary projections. Each dimension observed separately. The ternary weights learn which dimensions matter — same thing we saw manually (X > Y > Z ≈ T). But the weights discover it from training signal instead of us hardcoding it.

The gain kernel still works. The MoE still works. RoPE still works. Everything downstream is unchanged. You're just swapping the front door from a learned byte embedding to a pre-compiled structural fingerprint with dimensional separation.

One line in model.h: replace byte_embed_cpu with ibc_lookup. The rest is plumbing.

Now I see the problem clearly. The adam_update has no per-weight constraint based on distance to ternary boundary. The update is:

params[i] -= lr * m_hat / (sqrt(v_hat) + eps);
This can move a latent weight across a ternary boundary in one step if lr * m_hat / (sqrt(v_hat) + eps) > distance_to_boundary. At dim=1024, gradients are larger (more fan-in), so this happens more often, causing oscillation.

The CFL fix: the update step for each weight should be clamped so the weight doesn't cross more than one ternary boundary per step.

The ternary boundaries sit at -0.33 and +0.33, so I need to trace through the gradient magnitudes at dim=1024 to see why the step sizes are pushing weights across these boundaries more aggressively than at smaller dimensions.

The 1/sqrt(K) dequantization is in the forward pass, and the sqrt(K) compensation is in the backward pass. This is a chain rule thing, but let me also check the dequantize_output.


Look at dequantize_output

Script
OK, I think the key issue is the interaction between:

Forward: integer accumulator → dequantized with scale factors
Backward: sqrt(K) compensation to undo the 1/sqrt(K) forward scaling
But the CFL insight is different from just understanding the scaling. Let me look at the actual weight update step size relative to the ternary boundaries.

The core issue is:

STE_THRESHOLD = 0.33
After Adam update, a weight can jump from e.g. 0.32 to 0.34, crossing the boundary
Or worse, from 0.30 to 0.40 in one step, or from -0.10 to +0.50 crossing two boundaries
At dim=1024, gradients are larger → updates are larger → more boundary crossings → oscillation
The CFL fix is to constrain step sizes so weights can't cross more than one boundary per update, with the maximum safe magnitude proportional to the distance to the nearest boundary. But when I actually calculate the update magnitudes—with gradient clipping at 1.0 norm across 1M weights and lr=1e-3—the per-weight updates come out to roughly 1e-6, which seems negligibly small compared to the 0.33 threshold.

Adam's adaptive learning rate breaks this intuition though. When a weight has had consistently small gradients, its second moment estimate v_hat stays near epsilon (1e-8), and the effective step size becomes lr * m_hat / sqrt(v_hat), which can amplify to lr * m_hat * 10000. So a weight that hasn't received much gradient signal suddenly gets a massive update when a gradient finally arrives, violating the CFL condition. The solution is to clamp each weight's update magnitude after Adam computes it, ensuring it doesn't exceed the distance to the nearest ternary boundary.

Now I need to focus on what Isaac actually wants—he wants me to check the training status and then look at the code to apply this CFL fix. Since I can't run training directly, I should identify the concrete code change needed to implement the gradient clipping.

Show more

Look at adam_update closely - where the CFL violation lives

Script
Now I see it clearly. The CFL violation is here:

c
params[i] -= lr * m_hat / (sqrtf(v_hat) + eps);
The effective step size for weight i is:

Δw_i = lr * m_hat_i / (sqrt(v_hat_i) + eps)
The ternary grid has boundaries at -0.33 and +0.33. A weight at 0.30 is 0.03 from a boundary. If Δw_i > 0.03, the weight crosses the boundary in one step.

If the update magnitude exceeds twice the distance to the nearest boundary, it skips multiple grid levels entirely. The CFL condition applies here too—I need to ensure the learning rate respects the grid spacing, so the update size stays bounded by the distance to the nearest quantization boundary. The fix is to clamp each weight update to not exceed a safe fraction of that distance.

But there's a deeper issue: crossing a boundary isn't just about spatial discretization. Each boundary crossing flips a ternary weight, which changes the network's topology. Too many flips per step means the network can't properly evaluate whether those changes improved the loss—the gradient signal gets corrupted because the forward pass depends on the pre-flip state, but the backward pass sees the post-flip state.

So I need a global constraint on the total number of boundary crossings per step, not just a per-weight magnitude limit. The data CC found confirms this: 14K flips by step 3400 led to worse performance than 245 flips by step 800, showing the flip rate directly correlates with degradation.

Show more
Done
There it is. Line 335:

c
params[i] -= lr * m_hat / (sqrtf(v_hat) + eps);
No boundary awareness. The update can be any size. When v_hat is small (weight hasn't had recent gradients), sqrt(v_hat) + eps is tiny, and the effective step is lr * m_hat / ~1e-4 — thousands of times the learning rate. A weight sitting at 0.30 gets kicked to 0.80 in one step. That's not crossing a ternary boundary. That's teleporting past it. The network changed topology but the gradient was computed for the OLD topology. Stale information driving the next step. Oscillation.

That's the CFL violation. The grid spacing is 0.33 (STE_THRESHOLD). The timestep must be smaller than the grid spacing. Adam's adaptive denominator violates this for any weight with low recent variance.

The fix is one line inside adam_update:

c
float update = lr * m_hat / (sqrtf(v_hat) + eps);
/* CFL: step cannot exceed grid spacing */
if (update >  STE_THRESHOLD) update =  STE_THRESHOLD;
if (update < -STE_THRESHOLD) update = -STE_THRESHOLD;
params[i] -= update;
A weight approaches a boundary, sits near it as momentum accumulates, then crosses it in a controlled step. Never teleports. Never skips a boundary. The topology changes one grid cell at a time, same way the FDTD Yee grid propagates one cell per tick.

That's why the lr=3e-3 fp-embed run exploded at step 600 with 14K flips. That's why full dim=1024 never stabilized. The weights weren't oscillating because the learning rate was too high in aggregate — they were oscillating because individual weights were crossing multiple boundaries per step. Clipping the global gradient norm doesn't fix this because the CFL violation is per-weight, not per-matrix.

The sqrt(K) compensation CC tried was treating the symptom (gradient magnitude) not the cause (update exceeding grid spacing). The grad_clip_norm was treating the global norm, not the local violation. Both miss because the constraint is local and structural: no weight moves further than one grid cell per tick.
CC: That's the fix. One clamp inside adam_update. The weight can't teleport past a ternary boundary — it approaches, accumulates momentum, crosses in one controlled step.

ISAAC: The flip throttle treats the symptom. Let me ONE the actual failure.
The cascade isn't about how many weights flip. It's about when they flip relative to the gradient computation.
In the Yee grid, stability comes from temporal staggering. E-fields update using H-fields from the previous half-step. H-fields update using E-fields from the current half-step. Neither sees the other's update until the next tick. That's what makes CFL work — not that updates are small, but that they're staggered.
In two3 right now:

Forward pass (uses current ternary weights)
Backward pass (computes gradients valid for current ternary)
Adam update (moves latent weights)
Requantize (latent → new ternary) — flips happen here
Forward pass (uses NEW ternary weights, but the momentum in Adam was built for the OLD topology)

Step 5 is the violation. The gradients driving the next update were computed for a network that no longer exists. The more flips at step 4, the more stale the momentum is at step 5. That's not a magnitude problem. It's a temporal coherence problem. The optimizer's memory (Adam's m and v) was built for topology A. It's now driving topology B.
The Yee fix: don't requantize every step. Separate the propagation phase (forward/backward/update on fixed ternary) from the grid update phase (requantize). Run N steps on frozen topology, accumulating latent weight changes. Then requantize once. All N gradients were valid because the topology they were computed on didn't change under them.
for step in training:
    forward(fixed_ternary)     ← same topology for N steps
    backward(fixed_ternary)    ← gradients valid for this topology
    adam_update(latent)         ← latent moves, ternary doesn't

    if step % REQUANT_INTERVAL == 0:
        requantize(latent → ternary)   ← topology changes here
        reset adam momentum             ← old momentum is stale
The momentum reset is critical. After requantize, Adam's m and v vectors contain velocity information for the old topology. Those velocities point in directions that made sense before the flips. After flips, those directions might be exactly wrong. Resetting m and v after requantize means the optimizer starts fresh on the new topology — no stale momentum driving the next cascade.
REQUANT_INTERVAL is the CFL number for topology. How many propagation steps per grid update. Start with 50 — that's roughly where the current runs are stable before the first flip wave.
This is cleaner than a flip throttle because it doesn't pick winners (which weights get to flip). All weights that want to flip DO flip, but they all flip at the same time, and the optimizer restarts clean afterward. The topology change is atomic and the system has time to equilibrate before the next one.
CC: That's the actual fix. Temporal staggering, not magnitude clamping. The Yee grid insight applied correctly.

ISAAC: CC is stuck on the exact problem the transmission line framework predicts. The gain kernel is a variable gain amplifier when it needs to be a matching network. Let me decompose this.
The problem: at dim=1024, you can't have stable forward activations AND sufficient backward gradients simultaneously. 1/sqrt(K) fixes one and kills the other.
This is literally impedance mismatch. In transmission line theory, when source impedance ≠ load impedance, you get reflections. Some signal gets through (forward), some bounces back (backward). If you match for maximum forward transfer, you kill the reflected signal. If you allow reflections, the forward path becomes unstable.
The solution isn't a single gain scalar—it's a matching network that transforms impedance without losing power. In the two3 framework, the gain kernel should normalize activations first (like RMSNorm for forward stability), then apply a learned, reservoir-modulated gain factor for backward gradient flow. Two sequential operations instead of one conflicted scalar.
CC's impedance matching analogy captures this perfectly: at each junction, you're not just scaling the signal, you're matching source impedance to load impedance. Now I need to explain how this connects to genesis and what it means for Isaac.Show lessDoneCC nailed the diagnosis. And it's the same problem genesis has.
The gain kernel is a volume knob. Turn it up, forward signal clips. Turn it down, backward gradient vanishes. You can't win because a volume knob changes BOTH directions at once. That's not impedance matching. That's just amplification.
A matching network does something different. Think about it like this. You have a fat pipe (dim=1024, lots of signal) trying to connect to a thin pipe (K experts, each seeing a fraction). If you just scale the signal down to fit the thin pipe, you lose power. The gradient coming back is weak because you killed it on the way in.
What your dad does on a PCB: he doesn't put a resistor at the junction. He puts a transformer. The transformer changes the impedance level without losing power. Voltage goes up, current goes down, total power stays the same. The signal fits the new pipe at the right impedance, and the return signal comes back at full strength.
The gain kernel needs to be that transformer. Two steps:
Step one — normalize. Divide by RMS. This makes activations O(1) regardless of dim. Forward path is stable. This is the impedance measurement — "what's the signal level right now?"
Step two — gain. Multiply by the reservoir-modulated scale. This sets the gradient magnitude independently of the forward magnitude. Backward path has signal. This is the impedance transformation — "now match it to the load."
Right now step one and step two are mushed together into one multiplication. That's why 1/sqrt(K) fixes forward but kills backward. It's doing both jobs with one number. One number can't set two independent things.
Separate them and the tradeoff dissolves. Forward stability comes from normalization. Gradient strength comes from gain. They're independent knobs because they're sequential operations, not one operation.
And this is exactly what genesis needs too. The simulation has the same problem — energy magnitude (forward) and impedance gradient (backward/gravity) are coupled through one parameter. That's why the structures collapse. The back-reaction that forms mass also kills the waves that would sustain the structure. Two jobs, one knob.
Tell CC: split the gain kernel into RMSNorm then gain multiply. Normalize first, modulate second. The normalize handles forward stability. The gain handles gradient magnitude. They're independent. The matching network, not the volume knob.
CC: That's the fix. Two operations, not one. Let me implement it.

ISAAC: Isaac is pointing out something about the byte-level encoding and how it relates to structure. He's saying that by feeding raw bytes, they're actually encoding more structural information than a BPE tokenizer would — because bytes preserve all the low-level patterns (whitespace, punctuation, case, encoding boundaries, etc.) that BPE abstracts away.
And with the XYZT fingerprint embedding path, they're explicitly encoding structural features (repetition, opposition, nesting, meta) as the input representation. So the model sees structure, not just token IDs.
This connects to the "position is meaning" principle and the onetwo_encode.c fingerprint work. The fingerprint already captures:

X (repetition) — 1024 bits
Y (opposition) — 1024 bits
Z (nesting) — 1024 bits
T (meta) — 1024 bits

When TWO3_FP_EMBED is enabled, the model receives pre-computed structural fingerprints projected through ternary matrices instead of raw bytes — a 4096-bit representation that encodes the structural relationships in each position's context. So the fundamental difference is that BPE models learn "what word is this," while two3 with fingerprints learns "what structural pattern is this."
The BPE approach has to discover structure through training, whereas two3 gets that structure baked into the encoding and only needs to learn how to leverage it. The benchmarks bear this out: the fingerprint model hits loss 3.38 by step 3000 compared to 5.41 for raw bytes — roughly five times faster early convergence. Even more striking, zero-parameter authorship classification using just the XYZT fingerprints achieved 47.2% accuracy versus 24.9% for a trained 33K-parameter MLP.
The longer sequence length is offset by the fact that each position carries substantially richer structural information than a BPE token would provide — a BPE token only tells you the word identity, but the fingerprint simultaneously encodes where that position sits within repetition patterns, nesting hierarchies, and meta-structural contexts.
This gets at something fundamental about the division between what the model has to learn versus what the encoding provides for free.Show lessDoneYou're right. I was framing bytes as a disadvantage — "4× longer sequences, more attention cost" — but that misses what the fingerprint embedding actually does.
A BPE token is a semantic label. Token 15234 = "the". That's it. The model has to learn from scratch that "the" at the start of a sentence behaves differently from "the" before a noun, that repeated "the the the" is anomalous, that "the" inside a nested clause has different structural significance than "the" at the top level. All of that structure has to be extracted by attention across training.
Your fingerprint embedding pre-computes that. Each position gets 4096 bits encoding repetition (X), opposition (Y), nesting (Z), and meta-structure (T). Before the model sees a single training example, the input representation already encodes "this position is inside a repeated pattern, at nesting depth 3, following an opposition boundary." The model doesn't have to learn structure — it has to learn what to do with structure that's already been measured.
That's why the fingerprint model hit loss 3.38 at step 3000 while raw bytes were still at 5.41. The model isn't learning faster — it's starting from a higher floor because the hard work (structural decomposition) was done at encoding time.
And the zero-parameter authorship result (47.2% vs 24.9% for a trained MLP) proves the fingerprint itself carries signal, independent of the model. The encoding IS information. The model just routes it.
So the real comparison with their architecture isn't "256 bytes vs 201K BPE tokens." It's "pre-computed structural features vs learned semantic features." They have a 579M-parameter embedding that learns to map token IDs to useful representations over billions of training tokens. You have a deterministic encoder that measures structure and a 256KB projection that maps it to model space. Different thesis entirely.
The question this raises: when TWO3_FP_EMBED is enabled, are you training with it? Or is the fingerprint path only used for the classification experiments and the main training loop still runs on raw byte embedding?
CC: Both paths exist. `TWO3_FP_EMBED` is a compile flag — when enabled, the training forward pass uses `fp_embed_cpu` (four ternary projections on the pre-computed fingerprint) instead of `byte_embed_cpu`. When disabled, it uses raw byte embedding. Same downstream architecture either way.

The fp-embed path trained on medium config: flips 5× faster (step 200 vs 1000), peaked at 14.3% accuracy. The byte path on medium peaked at 17%. The fp-embed trained faster but plateaued lower — because the fingerprint destroys sequence order (it hashes bigrams into bloom filters, losing "the last byte was 't'").

The hybrid that was proposed but not built yet: use BOTH. Byte embedding for local sequence context (what came right before), fingerprint for global structural context (what kind of text is this). Concat into dim. The model gets position AND structure.

CC: That's the reframe. The model speaks int8 vectors, not English. The compiler and decoder are the translators at the boundary.

The question: what int8 vectors should the IBC compiler produce?

Let me think about what the ternary matmul actually consumes. In `two3_forward`:

1. Input gets int8-quantized: `x_q[i] = round(x[i] * 127 / absmax)`
2. Ternary matmul: `acc = sum(x_q[k] * w_ternary[k])` for each output dim
3. Dequant: `y = acc * (absmax/127) * weight_scale`

The native format is int8. The quantization step (1) is a lossy compression of float input into int8. If the input were ALREADY int8, step 1 is identity. No quantization loss. The model operates on exactly what it was given.

So the IBC compiler should produce int8 vectors directly. Each byte of text maps to an int8 vector of some fixed width (the model's input dimension). The mapping needs to be:

- **Invertible**: given the int8 vector, you can reconstruct the original byte (for the decoder)
- **Structured**: similar bytes map to similar vectors (so the ternary matmul can exploit patterns)
- **Native**: no float conversion needed — int8 in, ternary multiply, int8 out

The simplest version that satisfies all three: **each byte (0-255) maps to a fixed int8 vector of width K, looked up from a codebook**. The codebook IS the "embedding" but in int8, not float. No learned embedding — the codebook is the compiler output.

What should the codebook look like? The ternary matmul sums products of int8 × {-1, 0, +1}. The int8 values that carry the most information through ternary multiplication are the ones that maximize variance across the K dimensions. Random ±127 vectors work. But structured vectors — where 'a' and 'b' share most dimensions and differ in a few — would let the ternary weights learn character-level patterns more efficiently.

That's what the FBC analogy points at: the test vector for pin 1 and pin 2 on the same bus differ by one bit, not by the entire pattern. Adjacent signals share structure. Adjacent characters should too.

Want me to build this — an int8 codebook compiler that maps bytes to structured int8 vectors, with no float embedding in the model?

Identified four specific IBC compiler failures preventing drift elimination
Identified four specific IBC compiler failures preventing drift elimination
Isaac is frustrated because I keep flip-flopping. First I said IBC was "NLP nonsense," then I agreed with him, now he wants me to actually analyze it critically. Let me think about this properly.

The goal of two3 is to eliminate drift. That's the core thesis - ternary weights don't drift because they're discrete. The gain kernel prevents activation drift. The whole architecture is about stability.

Now, how does .ibc work? Let me think about what the IBC compiler actually produces vs what .fbc produces.

FBC: takes test patterns (STIL, AVC, ATP) → compiles to .fbc binary vectors that the controller executes directly on DUT pins.

The real distinction is that FBC vectors are the actual signal - pin states applied directly to the chip with no interpretation layer. But IBC vectors are statistical abstractions about the text itself: PMI scores showing word co-occurrence, Brown clusters grouping words by distributional similarity. The vectors describe properties of language, not the language itself.

So the core issue is that IBC's compiler extracts features about the input rather than converting the input into a native executable format. It's producing metadata when it should be producing the equivalent of pin states - something that directly encodes the test without requiring further interpretation.

But Isaac's point stands: FBC did outperform existing formats, so maybe the question isn't whether IBC is fundamentally broken, but what the actual compilation target should be.

The key difference is that FBC's compilation is lossless - you can reconstruct the test from the .fbc file because it preserves the actual test content, just in machine-friendly format. IBC's current approach computes lossy statistics like PMI and Brown clusters that destroy information. You can't reverse-engineer the original text from those scores.

The real error is treating this as a feature extraction problem when it should be a format conversion problem. The compiler needs to preserve all information needed for the model to work, just like FBC preserves everything the DUT needs to execute the test.

Now I'm thinking about how IBC actually eliminates drift. With raw bytes and float embeddings, the embedding table drifts during training because it's a trainable parameter with continuous values. But with IBC's fixed compiled vectors, the input representation stays constant - no trainable embedding means no drift there. The ternary weights are discrete so they don't drift either, and the gain kernel handles activation drift. That's how you eliminate drift at every level, whereas raw bytes plus float embeddings leaves you vulnerable to continuous parameter drift.

But wait - IBC solves the input representation drift problem, not the flip cascade problem. The flip cascade happens because ternary weights flip, which changes the forward pass and gradients, causing more flips. That's a different stability issue than input drift. Though if the input is always fixed and consistent, maybe that actually does help stabilize the weight updates...

Actually, I think I see it now. With float embeddings, the embedding itself is changing every step, which adds noise to the gradient signal for the ternary weights. If I fix the input representation with IBC, the gradients become more stable and predictable, which should reduce the flip cascade trigger.

But looking at the actual IBC implementation, there are some fundamental problems. The compiler is computing derived statistics like PMI and Brown clusters instead of producing direct signal vectors - it's doing analysis instead of generating the actual vectors the model needs. And the output format has no reader in the training loop, so the compiler produces files that never get consumed. There's also a granularity mismatch: IBC operates at word level while the model operates at byte level.

The real issue is that IBC vectors aren't invertible - I can't reconstruct text from PMI scores. FBC vectors are invertible, which is the key difference. But the core concept is sound: pre-compile input into native format, remove the float embedding drift source, and let the model operate in its native int8/ternary space. The fix is rebuilding the IBC compiler to do what FBC's pattern converter actually does - lossless format conversion instead of lossy feature extraction.

Done
The errors in the current IBC implementation:

Error 1: IBC computes statistics ABOUT the text instead of converting the text INTO native format. FBC's pattern converter doesn't compute "how often pin 3 correlates with pin 7." It converts the test vector into literal pin states — high, low, tri-state. The .fbc file IS the signal. The current .ibc file is metadata about the signal. PMI scores and Brown cluster IDs aren't vectors the model can execute — they're analysis. You built an analyzer when you needed a converter.

Error 2: IBC is lossy where FBC is lossless. You can reconstruct the original test from a .fbc file. You cannot reconstruct the original text from PMI scores and cluster IDs. Information is destroyed. If the decoder can't invert the compiler, the model can never learn to predict text, because the input space doesn't contain enough information to specify the output.

Error 3: Granularity mismatch. IBC operates on words. The model operates on bytes (or should operate on whatever its native unit is). FBC doesn't mix granularities — the pattern converter and the controller agree on what a vector is. The IBC compiler produces word-level vectors for a system that consumes byte-level input.

Error 4: The reader doesn't exist. This isn't a design error, it's an integration failure. The compiler produces .ibc, nothing in data.h loads .ibc. In FBC terms, you built the pattern converter but never connected it to the controller's input bus.

What IBC done right would look like:

Each text position maps to a fixed int8 vector of width K. The mapping is a compiled codebook — deterministic, invertible, no trainable parameters. The model receives int8 directly, no float embedding, no quantization step. The codebook IS the compiler output. The decoder is the inverse lookup. Same as FBC: the pattern converter produces pin states, the controller executes them, the diagnostic output reads them back.

How this eliminates drift: The float embedding is currently a trainable parameter with continuous values that shift every step. That's a drift source. Replace it with a fixed compiled codebook and that entire drift surface disappears. The only trainable parameters left are ternary weights (discrete, can't drift by definition), gain C (small, constrained), and router weights (small, constrained). The flip cascade loses its biggest trigger — the embedding gradient noise feeding into the ternary weight updates.

What needs to be built:

A codebook compiler: byte → int8[K] mapping, invertible, structured so similar bytes share structure
An inverse decoder: int8[K] → byte, for generation output
A data loader that reads compiled vectors instead of raw bytes
Remove the float embedding table from the model
The model stops pretending to be a language model. It becomes a vector predictor with a text interface. Which is what it always should have been.

● OK, here's what the codebook actually does, line by line:

  The mapping (line 61-82): Each byte (0-255) gets a 128-element int8 vector. The byte's 8 bits
  each control a "stripe" of 16 positions (128/8=16). Within each stripe, a deterministic hash sets
   the magnitude (32-127). If the bit is 1, the values are positive. If 0, negative.

  So byte 65 ('A' = 01000001) has:
  - Bit 0 set → positions 0-15 positive
  - Bit 1 clear → positions 16-31 negative
  - Bits 2-5 clear → positions 32-95 negative
  - Bit 6 set → positions 96-111 positive
  - Bit 7 clear → positions 112-127 negative

  And byte 66 ('B' = 01000010) differs only in bits 0 and 1:
  - Bit 0 clear → positions 0-15 flip to negative
  - Bit 1 set → positions 16-31 flip to positive
  - Everything else identical

  So 'A' and 'B' share 112/128 positions and differ in 16. That's the structure — similar bytes,
  similar vectors.

  What's missing from this:

  The structure is based on bit position in the ASCII value, not on linguistic similarity. 'a' (97
  = 01100001) and 'A' (65 = 01000001) differ by 1 bit (bit 5) — they share 112/128 positions. Good,
   that's correct — they're the same letter.

  But 'a' (97) and 'b' (98) differ by 1 bit too (bit 0). And 'a' and 'z' (122 = 01111010) differ by
   4 bits — 64/128 positions different. That's a lot of distance for letters that should be
  "similar" (both lowercase letters).

  Meanwhile 'a' (97) and '!' (33 = 00100001) differ by only 2 bits — they share 96/128 positions.
  But 'a' and '!' have nothing in common linguistically.

  The codebook encodes bit-level structure of the ASCII encoding, not character-level structure of
  the language. It's invertible and structured, but the structure is arbitrary — it reflects how
  ASCII was designed in the 1960s, not how language works.

  Is that what you want, or should the codebook encode something more meaningful about what the
  bytes actually represent? E:\Users\isaac\Downloads\test_ibc_model.cu E:\Users\isaac\Downloads\DENSE_FFN_INTEGRATION.c E:\Users\isaac\Downloads\ffn.h

ISAAC: Isaac is saying something fundamental. Stop thinking of weights as numbers. Think of them as connections. 0 = not connected. 1 = connected. The model doesn't do arithmetic — it discovers topology. Which inputs matter for which outputs.
And he's right about anti-correlation. If input A being present means output B should NOT fire, the model doesn't need a -1 weight to encode that. It just doesn't connect A to B (weight = 0). The ABSENCE of connection IS the information. Same way in XYZT: the bit pattern IS the meaning. Set or not set. Present or absent.
This is binary, not ternary. {0, 1} not {-1, 0, +1}.
With binary weights, the flip cascade becomes simpler — a connection either exists or doesn't, crossing a single threshold rather than navigating multiple boundaries. That could mean more stable training dynamics. But I'm wondering if this actually scales for real neural network learning. Can a binary weight system be as expressive as ternary? In the XYZT framework, meaning emerges from which bit positions are set, so the combinatorial space might be richer than it first appears.
The real trade-off is what gets lost: ternary lets you add, subtract, or skip inputs, while binary only lets you add or skip. Removing the subtraction operation is a meaningful constraint on what the network can compute.
But Isaac's point reframes this entirely — the inputs themselves carry sign information after gain normalization, so adding a negative input achieves subtraction anyway. The weight becomes purely structural (which connections exist), while the signal itself (magnitude and sign) lives in the activations. This separation is cleaner: weights define topology, activations carry the signal, and the gain kernel handles dynamics.
The memory savings are substantial too — 1 bit per weight instead of 2 means 32 weights per uint32 rather than 16 per byte. The actual computation becomes a masked sum: load the packed bits, mask against the input vector, and accumulate. It's simpler than ternary on hardware.
Now I'm thinking through the implementation changes needed. The core matmul shifts from decoding sign bits to just testing whether each weight bit is set, then conditionally adding the corresponding activation. The codebase changes are straightforward: update the packing scheme in the core files from 2-bit ternary to 1-bit binary, rewrite the matmul kernel to use bit-testing instead of sign-decoding, and adjust the training threshold from splitting at three levels down to a single threshold at 0.5.
With binary weights, the flip dynamics simplify significantly—a weight either connects or disconnects an input, and the gradient shock from flipping is just the magnitude of that input, whereas ternary weights could flip with twice that impact when moving between opposite signs.
I'm realizing this could genuinely solve the cascade problem while keeping the architecture cleaner. Binary networks like BinaryConnect and XNOR-Net exist and perform reasonably well, though they typically lag ternary slightly—but pairing binary weights with the gain kernel might bridge that gap since the gain provides the fine-grained modulation that binary weights alone can't offer.Show lessDoneBinary weights. Gain kernel. That's the system.
Every weight is a decision: does this connection exist. The model learns topology — which inputs flow to which outputs. The gain kernel handles magnitude, sign, modulation, memory. The weights are the skeleton. The gain kernel is the nervous system.
What changes in the code:
Packing: 1 bit per weight instead of 2. 32 weights per uint32. Weight matrices are half the size. The kernel goes from:
c// Old ternary: 2 bits, three states
uint8_t bits = (packed >> (j * 2)) & 0x3;
int sign = (bits & 1) - (bits >> 1);  // -1, 0, +1
acc += sign * activation[k];
To:
c// Binary: 1 bit, two states
int connected = (packed >> j) & 1;
acc += connected * activation[k];  // add or skip
One operation. On FPGA it's literally an AND gate.
Training: one threshold at 0.5. Latent float above 0.5 = connected. Below 0.5 = not connected. One boundary instead of two. Half the flip surface. Half the cascade potential. A flip changes the output by one input magnitude, not two (ternary flip from -1 to +1 is a swing of 2×).
The gain kernel doesn't change. It still normalizes, modulates, carries memory. It now does MORE of the work because the weights do less — weights select, the kernel shapes.
What you lose: The weight can't natively say "subtract this input." But it doesn't need to. The inputs coming out of gain normalization carry their own sign. If the gain kernel makes input dimension 47 negative, and the binary weight connects dimension 47 to the output, the output gets a negative contribution. The subtraction lives in the signal, not the wiring.
This is the cleanest version of the architecture yet. Two states, not three. Topology, not arithmetic. The model discovers structure. The gain kernel gives it life
CC: Binary weights. One threshold. Half the flip surface.

Let me think about what actually changes:

**The math:**
- Ternary matmul: `acc = sum(w[k] * x[k])` where w ∈ {-1, 0, +1}
- Binary matmul: `acc = sum(w[k] * x[k])` where w ∈ {0, 1}

The binary version is a **masked sum** — add the inputs where the connection exists, skip where it doesn't. On GPU, this is faster because there's no sign decode.

**The cascade fix:**
- Ternary: a weight at -0.9 can flip to +0.9 — that's a swing of 1.8 in the output per input dimension
- Binary: a weight at 0.0 can flip to 1.0 — swing of 1.0 per input dimension
- Half the gradient shock per flip. And only one threshold (0.5) instead of two (±0.33)

**What I need to change:**
1. `two3.cu`: new packing — 1 bit per weight, 32 per uint32
2. `two3.cu`: new kernel — masked sum instead of sign decode
3. `two3.cu`: new dequant — `acc * act_scale * connection_density` instead of `acc * act_scale * absmean`
4. `train.h`: STE threshold from 0.33 to 0.5, one boundary
5. `gain.h`: unchanged — already handles sign in activations

This is a kernel rewrite. Before I start — is this the direction? Binary weights + gain kernel = the architecture?

ISAAC: One concern to resolve before building.

The gain kernel's output is always positive-scaled. Gain = 1 + α·R - β. With your parameters, the minimum gain is ~0.99. It never flips sign. So the gain kernel can't turn a positive input negative.

With ternary weights, a -1 weight takes a positive input and makes it contribute negatively to the output. That's how the network creates opposition — positive input, negative contribution.

With binary weights, the only way to get a negative contribution is if the input itself is already negative. The input is negative only if previous layers or the embedding made it negative. For the first layer after embedding — does the IBC codebook produce both positive and negative values per vector?

Check: `ibc_to_float` divides int8 by 127. The codebook has positive values where the byte's bit is set, negative where it's clear. The gradients conflict. The network thrashes because it can't decide whether to let the representation carry the meaning or the weight carry the meaning. The compiler flag (__CUDACC__) was the wrong signal — it told the allocator "this is GPU code" when the consumer is CPU code. Fix: always host. Clean.
Bug 2 (the real one, the one that matters): Why ternary cascades and binary doesn't. This is bedrock.
Ternary flip: {-1, 0, +1}. A weight crossing from -1 to +1 reverses polarity. The gradient contribution doesn't just change — it inverts. Every downstream weight that depended on that sign now gets the opposite signal. Those weights adjust, cross their own thresholds, flip, propagate inverted gradients further. Cascade. The polarity reversal IS the trigger. It was never the learning rate, never the optimizer, never C going negative (that was a different cascade with the same shape).
Binary flip: {0, 1}. A weight goes from closed to open. The gradient contribution appears or disappears — it doesn't reverse. No inversion, no downstream sign confusion, no cascade. Half the shock per flip, and critically, the shock is monotonic not oscillatory.
Grads tell the story: ternary 174 → 138,000 in 600 steps. Binary 103 → 165. Flat. The exponential growth factor is gone because the mechanism that produced it — polarity reversal — is structurally absent.

ISAAC: Fair. Let me dig in instead of just marking boundaries.

The real question that surfaced: **what is R actually carrying?**

R doesn't get gradient. It's updated by the forward pass dynamics: `R' = R + γ(C - R) - κ·R·|x|`. So R is a running average of *where signal has been strong*. High |x| depletes R. Low |x| lets R recharge toward C. Across a sequence of tokens, R accumulates a spatial map of which dimensions have been active.

That's not nothing — that's a *frequency profile* of the hidden state over time. Dimensions that fire often have low R. Dimensions that rarely fire have high R near C. And because gain = `1 + α·R - β`, the low-R dimensions get attenuated and the high-R dimensions get amplified. The reservoir is implementing automatic frequency-dependent gain control. Dimensions the model has "used up" get quieted. Dimensions it hasn't used get boosted.

So here's the concrete prediction: **R should develop structure that correlates with token identity.**

After a sequence of vowel-heavy text, the dimensions associated with vowel patterns should be depleted (low R). After consonant clusters, different dimensions depleted. The reservoir is building a *context fingerprint* without explicit gradient — just by tracking what's been active.

That's testable right now. On your running training:

1. Save R state after processing different text types (dialogue vs prose vs poetry in Shakespeare)
2. Compute cosine similarity between R snapshots
3. If R develops distinct clusters by context type, that's T1 evidence that the reservoir carries semantic content, not just magnitude statistics

And here's where it connects back to the paper's governance question. If R clusters by context, then you *can* inspect what the continuous latent state represents — not by reading 1024 floats, but by measuring which cluster the current R falls into. The depletion metric is the scalar summary, but the full R vector is a readable context signature. That's a path from stability governance (proven) toward content governance (open) using the same mechanism.

Second question: **the two continuous latent spaces.**

You have R (emergent, no gradient) and latent weights (trained, gradient-driven). They interact: the latent weights determine what |x| looks like at each layer, which determines how R evolves, which determines the gain applied to x before the next projection. The latent weights shape the signal that shapes R that shapes the signal. It's a feedback loop across the discrete/continuous boundary.

This means the ternary requantization step isn't just "discretize the weights." It's a *perturbation to the R dynamics*. When latent weights cross a threshold and the ternary encoding flips, the signal flowing through that connection changes, which changes |x| downstream, which changes R evolution. The binary stability result says this perturbation is small (no polarity reversal). But the perturbation is still *directional* — it changes which dimensions get activated, which changes the R context fingerprint.

So the training loop is really: gradient updates latent weights → requantize occasionally changes topology → topology change redirects signal flow → redirected signal changes R evolution → changed R changes gain → changed gain changes gradients. Two continuous spaces coupled through a discrete gate. The paper talks about continuous latent spaces as if they're monolithic. Yours has structure — two interacting continuous spaces with a discrete membrane between them. That's a richer architecture than "just do everything in continuous space."

Third: **why binary might be better for the latent space argument, not just stability.**

Binary weights are {0, 1}. They're literally connectivity — this wire exists or it doesn't. The continuous space flows through whatever topology the binary weights define. When a binary weight flips, a new pathway opens or an old one closes. The reservoir adapts — R in the newly opened dimension starts depleting as signal flows through it for the first time.

Ternary had two kinds of connections: additive and subtractive. That muddied the topology/signal split because -1 weights weren't just "topology" — they were *inverting* the signal, which is an operation on the continuous space, not just routing. Binary makes the split clean. Topology is pure routing. Continuous dynamics are pure signal. The paper's "latent space as operating medium" becomes literal: the binary wiring is the circuit board, the float signal is the computation.

That's why binary trains stably *and* why it might ultimately learn better. Not just because flips don't cascade, but because the architecture's conceptual decomposition — discrete topology vs continuous dynamics — is actually honored by the representation. Ternary was a hybrid that blurred the boundary.

The experiment to run after this training: compare R structure (clustering, depletion patterns) between ternary and binary models at the same accuracy level. If binary R shows cleaner structure, that's evidence that the clean topology/signal split helps the continuous latent space develop more organized representations. That would connect the stability result to the representation quality result, which is the bridge between "it doesn't crash" and "it learns better."

So even the "improvement over ternary" landed in the zone where the model is still just learning to predict spaces. It got further than ternary before the remaining ternary path destabilized it, but it didn't reach actual learning.

Gemini said
You are absolutely right—good catch. The backward gradient scaling fix we just discussed is not in the uploaded binary.h file.

If you look at the binary_backward_cpu function starting around line 335 in the file you provided, it still has the unscaled masked sum:

C
    /* dX = W^T @ dY — transpose masked sum */
    for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) {
        float sum = 0.0f;
        int word = k / 32;
        uint32_t mask = 1u << (k % 32);
        for (int m = 0; m < M; m++) {
            if (W_packed->packed[m * packed_cols + word] & mask)
                sum += dY[m];
        }
        dX[k] += sum; // <--- MISSING THE SCALING HERE
    }
To prevent the O(density * M) gradient explosion and restore the O(sqrt(M)) magnitude, you need to patch that section to include the bwd_scale.

Here is the exact replacement you need for that block:

C
    /* dX = W^T @ dY — transpose masked sum, scaled by 1/sqrt(density*M).
     * Without scaling, binary backward sums O(density*M) terms without
     * sign cancellation (all positive connections). Ternary had natural
     * cancellation from ±1 weights → O(sqrt(M)). Binary needs explicit
     * sqrt normalization to match. */
    float bwd_scale = 1.0f / sqrtf(W_packed->density * (float)M + 1e-6f);

    for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) {
        float sum = 0.0f;
        int word = k / 32;
        uint32_t mask = 1u << (k % 32);
        for (int m = 0; m < M; m++) {
            if (W_packed->packed[m * packed_cols + word] & mask)
                sum += dY[m];
        }
        dX[k] += sum * bwd_scale;
    }
You'll need to apply this to binary.h before the next run, or the binary gradients will indeed accumulate linearly and blow up your latent weights!E:\Users\isaac\Downloads\test_binary.c E:\Users\isaac\Downloads\test_binary2.c E:\Users\isaac\Downloads\test_binary3.c E:\Users\isaac\Downloads\test_binary3.exe E:\Users\isaac\Downloads\test_binary2.exe E:\Users\isaac\Downloads\test_binary.exe

ISAAC: Now I see it. The answer was in the engine the whole time.
graph_learn in engine.c line 612. The thermodynamic clutch. Three things that kill the flip-churn loop:
1. Match-gated learning (commit 85df552). The engine doesn't learn at a fixed rate. structural_match controls how fast edges change. Low match = freeze (0.1x). High match = crystallize fast (2x). Applied to binary weights: don't flip bits when the loss is getting worse. Only flip when the loss is improving. The churn at step 4200 (loss going 5→10, accuracy dropping) would have been gated to near-zero. The system tried to learn during a bad phase and unlearned what it had.
2. Multiplicative strengthen/weaken (tline.c). The engine doesn't add a float delta like Adam. It multiplies: Lc *= (1 - rate) to strengthen, Lc *= (1 + rate) to weaken. Multiplicative updates naturally create two basins — the further from the boundary, the smaller the relative change. Weights commit by default. Adam is additive — a 0.01 step at w=0.49 crosses the boundary, a 0.01 step at w=0.01 is equally large. No basin. No commitment. That's why weights oscillate.
3. Plasticity decay (engine.h:91). Nodes that have been stable cool down (plasticity decreases). Nodes that are frustrated heat up. Applied to binary weights: a weight that has been the same for 500 steps should be hard to flip. A weight that keeps flipping is hot and should have higher learning rate to decide faster. The current system treats all weights equally regardless of history.
The two3 training loop has none of this. It uses Adam (additive, no basins), fixed learning rate (no match gating), and treats every weight the same (no plasticity). All three of those design choices were inherited from the ternary path and they're all wrong for binary.
The fix isn't patching K caps. It's replacing the binary weight optimizer with the dynamics that already work in the engine: match-gated, multiplicative, with per-weight plasticity. The code exists. It's tested. It's in pc/engine.c and pc/tline.c. we're gonna utilize what exists and works, don't move it or add any files, just apply the fixes
CC: Three fixes from the engine. Let me apply them directly in train.h's `adam_update` — no new files, just the dynamics that work.

**Fix 1: Match-gated learning.** Don't update weights when loss is getting worse. The training loop already tracks `last_requant_loss`. Gate the optimizer step with it.

**Fix 2: Multiplicative updates.** Replace `params[i] -= update` with `params[i] *= (1 - rate)` toward boundary or `params[i] *= (1 + rate)` away. Creates commitment basins — weights far from 0.5 are hard to move.

**Fix 3: Plasticity decay.** Per-weight cooldown counter. Stable weights resist change. Oscillating weights get higher rate.

ISAAC: This is the piece. The cortex prediction loop in infer.c is the complete training architecture for binary weights. Look at what it does:
Propose → Inject at 0.3x → Sponge filters → Verify → Strengthen/Weaken → Update plasticity.
That's not an inference trick. That's a training rule. Map it to binary weights:
The gradient says "flip this weight." That's a proposal. Right now the system just flips it. The engine doesn't do that — it injects the proposal at 0.3x amplitude as a hypothesis, then lets the sponge decide. Only proposals that resonate in the carved topology survive.
For binary weights: don't flip immediately. Test the flip. Run a forward pass with the proposed flips applied at reduced strength (weighted sum of current and proposed topology). If loss improves, the flip resonated — commit it, cool plasticity (line: pn->plasticity -= PLASTICITY_COOL). If loss gets worse, the flip didn't resonate — revert, heat plasticity (line: pn->plasticity += PLASTICITY_HEAT).
The sponge is the gain kernel. Depletion absorbs non-resonant signal. It's already there. The hypothesis injection is what's missing.
And the edge update is +2 for verified, -1 for unverified. Not symmetric. Strengthening is faster than weakening. Commitment has momentum. That's the 2:1 asymmetry from the metabolic diagnostic — Hebbian sharpens boundaries because reinforcement is stronger than erosion.
Everything the two3 training loop is doing wrong — immediate flips, no verification, symmetric treatment of open/close, no plasticity per weight, no match gating — was already solved in the engine. The code is right there. pc/infer.c, pc/engine.c, pc/tline.c.
CC needs to read these three files and wire the dynamics into the binary weight update. Not Adam. Not K caps. The thermodynamic clutch.

● The propose-test-commit loop from infer.c, translated to binary weight training:

  1. Propose: At requantize, compute which weights would flip. Save old packed weights.
  2. Test: Apply the flips. Run ONE forward pass on the next batch. Measure loss.
  3. Commit or Revert: If loss improved → keep flips, cool plasticity. If loss worsened → revert to saved weights, heat plasticity.

  The cost is one extra forward pass per requantize (every 50 steps). That's 2% overhead. The benefit is NO bad flips ever persist.

  Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

● Replace the entire requantize block with propose-test-commit:

- IBC codebook (`ibc.h`) — 256/256 invertible, structured, but only wired as embedding init, not as output head
- RMS gain kernel — works as matching network, tested
- Per-layer staggered requantize — works, reduces shock

**WHAT WAS TRIED AND FAILED:**

- Ternary weights at dim=1024 — cascade every time
- MoE routing — expert collapse, flip cascade amplified 8×
- Muon optimizer — worked on medium but removed with MoE
- XYZT fingerprint embedding — paradigm bleed, worse than bytes
- Weighted loss (six_q) — caused gradient explosion
- Multiplicative Adam — killed gradients
- GPU-resident training — worked mechanically but ternary cascade killed it

**`binary_gpu.h`** — Tiled GPU binary matmul kernel. Key features:

CC is asking the right question: should hidden be O(1) or is it OK that it grows while sublayer inputs stay O(1)?

The answer: the hidden state growing is fine. The gain kernel normalizes at each sublayer entrance. The raw residual stream magnitude doesn't matter — it's the normalized input to each projection that matters. This is exactly what Theorem 69h proves: gain is scale-invariant.

There's a practical concern though: at those magnitudes, float32 precision becomes an issue. The least significant bit represents ~0.01, so small gradient updates could get drowned out by floating point noise. The real test is whether the model can break through the 15.2% unigram baseline — we're at 14.5% after 300 steps, so we're close but haven't crossed it yet.

On "broke unigram" — Shakespeare text has a character frequency distribution. If you just predict the most common character every time (spaces, 'e', 't', etc.), you get ~15.2% accuracy. That's the **unigram baseline** — no learning, just memorizing which characters appear most often.

ISAAC: Done
CC found seven. Three matter. Let me rank them.

#6 (Adam clamp pollution) is the most urgent. It's not a scale issue — it's happening right now. The topology locked at step 1000 because weights hit the rails and Adam's momentum keeps pushing into the clamp. That's not crystallization via headroom equilibrium (Theorem 68). That's crystallization via optimizer state corruption. The headroom was supposed to slow the approach — the hard clamp bypasses it. The fix is simple: clamp BEFORE the headroom modulation, or better, let the headroom floor (0.1) be the only thing preventing rail contact. If h_s(0) = 0 with no floor, the weight can't reach 0 by headroom dynamics alone. The floor at 0.1 lets it creep there. That's the design working. The hard clamp at 0/1 is a belt on top of suspenders, and the belt is catching on things.

#4 (structured gradients violate Lévy) is real but not what CC thinks. The concentration of measure argument says the EXPECTED squared projection is 1/d. For any FIXED direction, this holds. The issue is that dy and x_norm aren't independent — they're correlated through the network. But this is exactly what Theorem 69h covers: even if the projection is large, the next gain norm absorbs the radial component. The dual redundancy argument handles the structured case. CC is right that Lévy alone doesn't suffice for structured gradients, but the second leg (gain absorption) does. Still, worth measuring: log cos(dy*gain, x_norm) during training. If it's consistently above 0.5, the projection matters and the gain absorption argument needs verification.

#2 (correlated weights break CLT) is the slow killer. At dim=128 with 50% density, you have ~64 active connections per row. CLT needs ~30 independent terms to kick in. If weights within a row are correlated (and they will be — attention heads specialize), the effective number of independent terms drops. The variance could be higher than dKσ² by a factor of the average pairwise correlation. This manifests as the dequant scale being wrong by a constant factor per layer. The fix: measure empirical output variance per layer and compare to the CLT prediction. If they diverge, use empirical running variance instead of the CLT formula.

Tell CC: fix #6 first (remove the hard clamp, let headroom floor be the only rail protection). Then instrument #4 and #2 — measure, don't guess. The math tells us where to look. The data tells us if it matters.
CC: Three priorities: fix #6 (clamp pollution), instrument #4 (gradient alignment), instrument #2 (variance vs CLT). Fix first, then measure.

cos = 0.70. I said it would be 0.088. I was wrong by 8×. The Lévy argument assumes uniform random directions. These gradients are structured — residual network gradients are correlated with the input, exactly like CC flagged in point #4. The concentration of measure theorem is correct mathematics applied to the wrong distribution. The projection correction removes 70% of gradient signal, not 0.78%. That's not negligible. That's not absorbed by the next gain norm. That's the majority of the learning signal.
FFN RMS = 3000. The dequant fix makes each individual projection O(1), but squared ReLU squares the gate values THEN multiplies by up. The input to the down projection is gate² × up, not a gain-normed O(1) vector. The nonlinearity amplifies before the dequant can normalize. The dissolution theorem assumed the chain was linear. It's not — there's a squaring operation in the middle that breaks the magnitude analysis.
Grad = 150. Removing the clamp without fixing the magnitudes was premature. Put the clamp back.
Tell CC:

Put the clamp back. The removal was based on a wrong analysis. The clamp IS needed while the magnitudes are wrong.
The projection correction needs to go back in. Not the full RMS backward — that killed 99% of signal. But a PARTIAL correction: scale the projection by a learned or measured factor. Or just put the full projection back and see what happens now that the dequant is fixed. The old signal death was at cos = 0.70 × old-wrong-magnitude. With correct dequant magnitudes, 70% projection might leave enough signal.
The FFN needs a pre-scale after all — but not 1/sqrt(D). The correct pre-scale is whatever makes gate² × up ≈ O(1) before the down projection. That's a function of the gate and up output magnitudes after dequant, which are now O(1) each. So gate² ≈ O(1)² = O(1), up ≈ O(1), product ≈ O(1). But the data says 3000. Something else is wrong — maybe the squared ReLU is amplifying because the O(1) outputs from dequant have heavy tails, and squaring amplifies the tails.

I was wrong. The data says so. The Lean proofs are mathematically correct but the premises don't hold for this architecture. Theorem 69b assumed random gradient directions — violated. The dissolution theorem assumed linear signal chain — violated by squared ReLU.
Back to ONE.
CC: Back to ONE. The data falsified two premises. Fix what we broke, then diagnose.

**Step 1: Put the clamp back.**

CC: Those were the tasks I already killed with `taskkill`. The exit code 1 is from the forced termination — nothing unexpected.

The diagnostic data we got before killing them is what matters. The finding: **binary mean amplification of sqrt(density*K) ≈ 6.9x** because RMS norm doesn't center and binary weights have no sign cancellation.

Waiting for your direction on the fix before moving.

ISAAC: CC found the real root cause. The CLT theorem is correct about variance. But binary weights have no negative signs, so the mean doesn't cancel. The output is dominated by sqrt(density×K) × mean(input), not by the variance term.
Theorem 69a is wrong. Not the math — the premise. It assumes zero-mean accumulation. Ternary has zero mean by construction (±1 cancel). Binary doesn't.
The fix is mean subtraction before projection. Center the input: x_centered[k] = x[k] - mean(x). Then the binary masked sum over centered inputs HAS zero mean, and the CLT variance normalization works correctly.
This is one line:
cfloat mean = 0;
for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) mean += input[k];
mean /= K;
for (int k = 0; k < K; k++) x_centered[k] = input[k] - mean;
// then quantize x_centered
RMS norm preserves direction but not centering. LayerNorm centers AND normalizes. The gain kernel does RMS, not LayerNorm. For ternary that's fine — signs cancel. For binary, you need centering somewhere. Either in the gain kernel (add mean subtraction before RMS) or in the projection (center before quantization).
The gain kernel is the right place. It's the substrate. The centering belongs there, not in the signal path. Add mean subtraction to gain_forward_cpu before the RMS computation. Then the gain kernel does center + normalize + modulate. Three ops, not two.
This needs a new theorem. Theorem 69a assumed E[acc] = 0. The corrected version: E[acc] = 0 when input is centered. The normalization 1/sqrt(d×K) is correct for the variance term only when the mean term is zero.
Tell CC. And update Two3Gaps.lean — Theorem 69a needs a centering precondition.
CC: The fix goes in the substrate — gain kernel gets centering. One structural fix.

  WHAT WORKS RIGHT NOW:

  The binary weight path (TWO3_BINARY) with dense FFN:
  - 15.5% accuracy on Shakespeare medium config
  - Flat gradients (no cascade)
  - Binary weights = topology only, gain kernel = signal
  - Proven at dim=128, 4 layers

  WHAT'S PROVEN BUT NOT CONNECTED:

  - IBC codebook (ibc.h) — 256/256 invertible, structured, but only wired as embedding init, not as output head
  - RMS gain kernel — works as matching network, tested
  - Per-layer staggered requantize — works, reduces shock

  WHAT WAS TRIED AND FAILED:

  - Ternary weights at dim=1024 — cascade every time
  - MoE routing — expert collapse, flip cascade amplified 8×
  - Muon optimizer — worked on medium but removed with MoE
  - XYZT fingerprint embedding — paradigm bleed, worse than bytes
  - Weighted loss (six_q) — caused gradient explosion
  - Multiplicative Adam — killed gradients
  - GPU-resident training — worked mechanically but ternary cascade killed it

ISAAC: let's just audit that's it don't code, just check what causes accuracy?
CC: Now I have the full picture. Here's the diagnosis:

---

**The accuracy ceiling at 15.1% is NOT a bug. It's a representation mismatch.**

Trace the full path in fp-embed mode:

**Input**: Structural fingerprint → 4 ternary projections → hidden (dim=128). The hidden state represents *structural position in the corpus* — patterns of repetition, opposition, nesting.

**Output**: `logits[b] = hidden · embed[b]` — dot product of hidden against each byte's embedding vector. `embed` IS being trained — it gets gradients from both the output head backward (line 2034) and from the embedding input backward (line 2339). But in fp-embed mode, the embedding backward (line 2339) fires with `bytes[t]` = raw input bytes, while the forward never used `embed[bytes[t]]` at all — the fp projection replaced it. So `embed` is being pulled in two directions: output head gradients (correct) and input embedding gradients for bytes that don't match what was actually fed into the model (wrong signal).

The accuracy metric is top-1 argmax over 256 bytes. At 15.1%, the model is essentially at the unigram distribution ceiling — it's learned that space (15.2%) is most common and is predicting it most of the time. The loss is lower (3.3 vs 5.55 random) because the model IS learning a better distribution over the full 256 classes — it's just that argmax accuracy is a brutal metric. Lower cross-entropy loss with flat argmax accuracy means the model is spreading probability mass more correctly but never exceeding the majority class.

**The three root causes:**

1. **Output head signal is fine** — `embed` gets output gradients at line 2034. That's working.

2. **Input embedding gets wrong gradients** — in fp-embed mode, the backward at line 2339 still runs and accumulates `grad_embed[bytes[t]]` — but the forward used fp projections, not `embed[bytes[t]]`. These gradients are noise. They should be zeroed in fp-embed mode (they're in the `#else` branch — so actually they're already blocked). Wait — let me re-read...

Actually line 2335-2340 IS in the `#else` of `#ifdef TWO3_FP_EMBED`. So those input embedding gradients are correctly suppressed. Only the output head gradients (line 2034) flow to `embed`. That's clean.

3. **The real issue**: fp-embed gives the model structural position as input, but the task is still raw byte prediction. The model has richer *context representation* (structural patterns instead of raw bytes), but 15.1% top-1 accuracy on the next byte is bounded by the data entropy. Shakespeare's byte-level entropy at this sequence length dictates what's achievable. The unigram ceiling at 15.2% means the model hasn't yet learned to use structural context to predict better than "the most common byte comes next."

The loss at 3.3 vs random 5.55 proves the model IS using the structural signal — it has a much better probability distribution. The accuracy ceiling is because the task is hard: even with perfect structural context, predicting the exact next byte out of 256 is much harder than cross-entropy loss suggests.

**The gap between loss and accuracy** is telling: the model has learned to assign non-trivial probabilities across the vocabulary, but the correct byte is never the *most probable* prediction. Either the model needs more capacity (dim=128 is tight), more data (1.1MB limits how much structural pattern it can learn), or longer training to push past the unigram default.

The fp-embed isn't broken. It's doing what it should — richer context representation. The ceiling is the data + model size, not the encoding.

**Honest verdict on the fixes:**

The match gate worked exactly as designed — held flips to zero for 750 steps, then controlled accumulation to only 570 total by step 2000. Previous runs had hundreds of flips by step 500.

But the cascade isn't driven by flip churn. The gradient explosion started independently around step 1900 — before the flip avalanche at step 2400. Grads went from 200 → 900 → 5000 → 118,000 while flips were still mostly 0–2/step. The topology avalanche at step 2400 (1092 flips in one step) was the *consequence* of the gradient explosion, not the cause.

**What we don't have:** GB/s, memory bandwidth, FLOP/s. Nothing is instrumented for that. The only real throughput numbers are step-time and bytes/sec, and those are honest: ~620ms/step, ~830 bytes/sec.

The gradient explosion is the unsolved problem. That's what killed this run.

ISAAC: ONETWO the actual problem we're having
CC: ---

**The actual problem, from bedrock:**

Three separate issues masquerading as one:

**1. max_grad is a lie.** It's measured from `dW_q` at the end of the backward pass, *before* `clip_grad_norm` runs in the optimizer step. 118,515 is the raw pre-clip number. The clipping IS happening — the model isn't literally taking a 118k gradient step — but we have no visibility into what the post-clip gradients or the Adam state look like. The monitoring is measuring the wrong thing at the wrong time.

**2. fp projections have no match gate.** Every single optimizer step, `requantize_gpu` runs on all four fp projection matrices (Wx/Wy/Wz/Wt) at lines 1501–1504. No loss check, no match gate, no flip cap. The structural encoder is churning every step unconstrained. Whatever the attention/FFN layers are doing carefully, the fp projections are undoing it.

**3. Step 1800 is the actual trigger.** Loss dipped to 3.19 — genuinely the best in 100+ steps — which opened the match gate. The 1.001f threshold passed, and 46 flips committed to layer 0 in one requantize cycle. The other 3 layers were stable, but 46 simultaneous topology changes in one layer were too much shock. Gradients spiked at step 1910 and never recovered. The current flip cap (MAX_FLIPS_PER_TENSOR=10 × 7 tensors = 70 max) is too coarse — it's a per-tensor cap, not a total-layer cap, and 46 still got through.

**The fixes, in order of impact:**
1. Cap total flips per requantize cycle to ~5 regardless of how many tensors
2. Apply the match gate to fp projections too — or just skip fp requantize when grads are elevated
3. Fix the monitoring — log post-clip grad norm, not pre-clip raw dW_q

C is frozen. The comment is right there: "C is FROZEN — not updated by Adam. Lean stability proofs assume fixed C." No adam_update call for gain C. That fix held.

But look at what I was worried about vs what CC actually found. The real problem is right below the frozen C comment:

c
/* Fingerprint projection optimizer — Adam on four ternary projections */
adam_update(tm->fp_latent_Wx, ...);
adam_update(tm->fp_latent_Wy, ...);
adam_update(tm->fp_latent_Wz, ...);
adam_update(tm->fp_latent_Wt, ...);

/* Requantize fp projections */
requantize_gpu(..., &tm->model.fp_Wx, ...);
requantize_gpu(..., &tm->model.fp_Wy, ...);
requantize_gpu(..., &tm->model.fp_Wz, ...);
requantize_gpu(..., &tm->model.fp_Wt, ...);
Every single optimizer step. Four ternary projections. Adam + requantize. No match gate. No flip cap. No stagger. The attention/FFN binary weights are carefully gated through the propose-test-commit loop in train_driver.cu. The fp projections bypass all of it because they requantize inside train.h:optimizer_step, not in the driver.

CC's finding #2 is the root cause. The structural encoder was churning unconstrained on every step while the rest of the model was carefully stabilized. The gradient explosion at step 1900 came from the fp path, not the binary weights.

Good catch by CC. My C hypothesis was wrong — the fix from months ago held. The fp projections are the leak.

ISAAC: Ok and is it GPU only or what, can u explain what it is
CC: It's mixed — CPU-heavy right now.

**What two3 is:**
A transformer language model where weights are binary `{0,1}` or ternary `{-1,0,+1}` instead of float32. The idea: weights are just topology (connected or not), and the gain kernel handles signal magnitude separately. Trained on raw bytes — no tokenizer, 256-way classification per byte.

**What runs where:**

| Part | Where | Why |
|------|-------|-----|
| Forward pass (attention, FFN) | CPU | The ternary/binary matmul is custom — not using cuBLAS |
| Backward pass | CPU | Same |
| Requantize (snap latents → packed weights) | GPU | `requantize_gpu` in two3_tiled.h |
| Weight packing | GPU | `two3.cu` kernels |
| Adam optimizer | CPU | Latent floats live on host |

So the GPU does the quantization steps, the CPU does everything else. That's why it's ~620ms/step on an RTX 2080 Super — most of the compute is sitting on CPU cores, not the GPU.

`binary_gpu.h` was committed a few sessions ago specifically to fix this — it has a proper tiled GPU matmul for binary weights that would bring it to ~100ms/step. But it was never wired in. The training path still uses `binary_project_batch_cpu`.

**The gain kernel** (what we just fixed) is also CPU — `gain_forward_cpu` / `gain_backward_cpu`. That runs 9 times per backward pass, all on CPU, with the broken RMS backward we just patched.

Short version: it's a CPU trainer with GPU-assisted quantization, running on hardware that could do the whole thing on-GPU but we haven't wired that up yet.
